These loading conditions are determining some elements\nof the payload structure design. Payloads that are required to he\ncompatible with both an e^rpendable launch vehicle and th^^ Shuttle will\nbe weight constrained by the expendable Launch vehicle. other payloads\nflown on interim upper stages launched Ervin the orbiter will be weight\nconstraiarAed by the expendable launch vehicle. Other payloads flown\non interim upper stages launched from the orbiter will be weight constrained by the performance of the IUS. In addition, the dynamic char acteristics of the orbiter are expected to be verified by ground attd\nflight tests during calendar years 1977 and 1978.\nIn view of the severe loads cnviran^-nent and the existence\nof weight constraints, the use of load alleviation concepts appears to\nbe attractive. These concepts have the following potential advantages\nover redesigning the structure: (1) the weight penalty to the payload\nis minimal, (2} if dynamic loads become a critical design problem\nLate in the payload development program, load alleviation devices can,\nin concept, be added with modifications to the payload design and testing program.\n\n,--.-,\n^:^ r\n\nZ-2\n\n^\t\n\n;; 3\n\nr^^\n\n.,\n,...\n\n\'"\t\n\nTo enable assessment of the feasibility of load allevia^\ntio^^ runc^^pts, the user will require a body of infar^mation on the effectiveHess of various allc^viatian approaches (e. g., additional payload supports,\nshock isolation, modification of the Shuttle operation} anti the effects\nan 5par.ccraft dc^vcic^pment, test, and operation.\t This information should\nbe contained v^ii:hin the users\' guide.\nZ. 1. ,\'\t\n\n-\t .\nr\n\nJustification\n\n"i.\n\nThere arc a variety of situations in which dynamic\nlaaci all^\xe2\x80\xa2viation devices could, in concept, be very advantageous to\na paylaacl.\t These situations include the following.\n1.\t\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\t\n\nZ.\t\n\n3.\t\n\nr^^^.\t\n,^\n^^\n\n,`\n\nA payload program, planning initial launches\non an expendable launch vehicle with later\ntransition to the Shuttle, must consider the\ndynamic lateral loads resulting from the Shuttle\nevents.\t Since these payloads were not designed\nfor the high lateral loads that can result from\nevents such as the Shuttle landing and liftoff\nconditions, some type of load alleviation scherine\nmay be required to avoid the modifications and\nadditional weight associated with strengthening\nof the spacecraft.\nIf the payload weight is limited by an expendable\nlaunch vehicle capability and structural beef-up\nis required far loads encountered during ascent\nar landing when the payload is in the Space\nShuttle, load alleviation devices can be used\nto minimize the payload weight change sa that\nit can remain compatible with the expendable\nlaunch vehicle.\t This is most likely to occur\nwith payloads transitioning from expendable\nlaunch vehicles to the Shuttle.\nWhen the payload weight is limited by the upper\nstage capability, load alleviation devices may\nbe desirable to minimize structural weight.\t This\nxnay happen on new payloads or an payloads\ntransitioning from an expendable launch vehicle\nto the Shuttle.\n\n^,\n^\n,,\n\'\t j\n?.:\n^\t :^\n\n^\t\n\n-\n\n;\n\n2-3\n\n^,`?\n\n^\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n.;\n^\' .\n\n`^\n\n4.\n\nIf the payload is designed fnr the orbiter using\npredicted dynamic loads which are later refined\nor revised so that the loads exceed the design\nloads, load alleviation devices may be used\nto avoid requalification at the higher load levels.\n\nThere are several general approaches to be investigated\nwhen payloads are designed for lands which prove to bc. non-conservative.\nFar example, a different landing technigL: e, i. e. , lower sink rate,\nmay be provided to reduce the landing load on the payload. Second,\nin order to carry the loads through the payload structure, a beef-up\nof the payload structure can usually be accomplished. Third, the use\nof land alleviation devices such as dampers, absorbers. and snubbers\ncan be utilized to reduce the loads due to dynamic amplification. In\norder t^ properly consider the latter alternatives and evaluate feasibility\nand costs, as well as impacts nn payload design and operation, data\nare needed by the user on potential load alleviation devices.\n\n2. 1. 3\t\n\nOperating Plan\n\n2.1.3.1\t\n\nTechnical Objective\n\nThe technical objective is to furnish information needed\nby the user on STS dynaxx^ic load alleviation concepts. Snme of\nthe requirements and considerations for such a design are as follows.\nI. The DSP should be decoupled mechanically Exam the\nTranstage during launch and landing so that, in the event\nof an abort, it is not required to move the payload\nxelative to the Transtage (Figure 10-7).\n2. Arnechanism is xequired which would hold the DSP\nto its cradle duri^^.g launch, flight, and landing, yet\nwould allow movement of the DSP relative to its cradle\nfor coupling or decoupling. Decoupled DSP/Transtage\n\n\t\n._,\t .\t\n\n.;\n\n_ .\t\n\n^.\t\n\n^\t\n\n-.\n\n.\xe2\x80\xa2r,\n\n4.\n\nThe DSP, as well as most other spacecraft designed\nfar launch on an expendable launch vehicle, is not designed\nto carxy Toads through any interface except the aft one.\nSupporting the DSP in its own cradle will. require not\nonly support at the aft end but also at some forward point\nsuch as the main body/sensor interface. The mass\nwould then be free to vibrate relative to the payload whenever the payload itself acted as the forcing function (see Figure l0-$).\nThis type of system is normally employed under conditions\nof steady state vibration when it is desirable to reduce the amplitude of\nthe vibrating object or, as i7 the case of a transient input such as that induced\nby a landing shock, it is desired to limit. the number of cycles the payload\nwould undergo. However, this system probably would not reduce the\ninitial acceleration the payload would experience on landing. In addition,\nthe mass required could possibly be a large percentage (IO to 20 percent}\nof the payload, In the case of the DSP, which weighs 2600 lb, the added\nweight could be appreciable. Structural modifications to the spacecraft\nmay also be required, For these reasons, this concept does not appear\nto be as attractive as others for further study.\n\n10-Z1\n\n11\n\n4a^\'iP\n\n^\n1\n\ni,\n\n1\ni#L\n^, ^.\t ^ j\t\n\n.^\n\n^ ^ E\t ,^\n,\t ,\n^\nd\n\n-..\t -\n\nI0.\n\n^..\n\n5\t\n\n^\n\n^\n\n^\n\nCOT1C Iusi oIls\n\nf`\n^\'\n^#\n\nBased on the invesfigation described in this section, there\nappear to be thxee different concepts which could be investigated as\npossible candidates for reducing the accelerations experienced by the\npayload in the orbiter bay during landing.\t These three concepfs are:\n\nF;\n\n^;.\t ;\n^.= f\nIr\t ^\n\n1.\t\n\nShock isolators employed between the baseline Transtage\nand its supporting cradle\n\n2.\t\n\nA stxongback extension added to the Transtage cradle\nto support the DSP\n\n3.\t\n\nDecou led-DSP/Txanstage.\np\t\n\n^.\n`\t ``\n^F\n\nConcept ^ could be ixryplerr^.ented with or without shock isolation.\t All\nthree concepts m.exit further study, includi3rxg a pxelirninar.y design\ndefir3itian analysis of fhe effectiveness of each in reducing xnaxirnum\ndesign loads and the applicability of each concept to STS payloads.\n\n.\t\n\n^:.\n\n3\t ^\t "\n\n!\t ^\t ^.\n^r:\n.\n^..\n\n^..\t\n\n^::\n\nK\n\nE\t\n\nData needed by the STS user regarding these concepts are\nweighf increment, range of reduction in rn,aximuxra loads (for ;varying\nfrequencies}, xnethad of attachment and release, requirements fox\nreattachment, dixx^.ensions, typical installafions, loads induced at attach\npt^int, and stiffness and damping characteristics of the device s`}^\n\nr\n\'\t ``....\n\n^\'\'\n^\'\n\ns^-;\n\ny\n\n^\n^\n\n;\'\n\n^_\n\n,.\n4.\t\n\n1\n1\n\n^^^^.^\n\n^.\n\nf\n._.^.`_\t\n\n.\t\n\n.\n\ni\n\n\t\n\n.\n..\t\n\n..\t\n\n---\t\n\n^\n\n10. Charts axe presented of payload capability\nto circular orbits as a function of orbit altitude and orbit inclination.\nDelivery only situations arP presented separately from those for deliver=^\nand rendezvous. Elliptic orbit capabilities for l^0-nxni perigees are\nalso presented. For these, landing opportunities are influenced by the\nrelative positions of the landing sites and orbit apsides; payloads corxespanding to the extreme cases are presented. In addition, the Air\nForce in the operation of its aircraft takes many precautions to linzi.t\nthe hazard and- annoyance of the general public. Far instance, Air Force\nRegulation 6D-5, Conduct of Air Missions Involving Hazardaas Carte,\ndirects that aircraft commanders will plan flight routing to minimize\nflights over heaYrily populated or otherwise critical areas. Hazardoa.s\ncargoes specifically defined in this regulation are nuclear weapons and\nhigh. explosives. However, the obvious intent could be applied to the\nreturn of the orbiter. Also, AFR 55-34, Reducing Flight Disturbances\nThat Cause Adverse public Reactions, provides practices -that should\nbe used in air aperati.ons involving supersonic flight because of the sonic\nboom problem.. These practices, if . applied to the orbiter, could be\nrestrictive in terms of orbit inclination. because of limited crassrange\nand maneuvering capability for Abort Once Around {AOA) and End of\nMission (EOM) .\nThe return flight of .the orbiter involves a relatively fixed\nflight plan because of -the need for precise energy managez7nent during\nthe entry and terminal flight phases. It also involves a severe reentry\nenviransnent including high levels of heating, high dynamic pressures,\nand a variety of atmospheric effects such as .aero-electrical phenomena,\nIn view of the position taken relative to hazardous operations and public\nreactions for aircraft operations, it is likely that constraints on orbiter\noperations during the entry and landizag phases xnay be established. Such\nconstraints, because of the linxited flexibility of the return flight plan\n\n1D-28\n\n...\n\n,_\n\n1\t\n-^\n\n;y\na\n\na\na\n\n^^\n^,...r\xc2\xb0\n\nfor the orbiter, could have a significant impact on a Shuttle user in terms\nof the launch azimuths and anclinataon angles that can be used. Therefore,\nthe Shuttle user should know during the planning stage of a prvgrarn\nwhether there are such constraints or not and if there are, the lizxaitaLions imposed on Shuttle missions should lae defined. \t\n10. 3.2\t\n\nr\n\nReference Document\n\nThe Space Shuttle .Launch and Recover y Site Review Soard\nreport (see Surraxnar^r, page 1\xc2\xa9-30} was reviewed. Zt was reported that\nthe capability to place the sonic boom footprint by changing reentry\ntrajectory and glide paths would minimize the potential restrictions\nduring normal return ar during abort. However, this conclusion needs\nto be clarified and defined sa that users can understand any entry andterminal flight phase constraints on the rnissaon.\nY\t\n\nl0. 3.3\t\n\nStudy Result\n\n,_\n\nNo solid evidence was found at this time showing that\nthere will be restrictions on return-flight corridors which will limit\nthe orbiter ephemeris (inclination or altitude). The number of normal\nreturn opportunities per day may possibly be .limited by the desire to\nnormally restrict the sonic booze nuisance to as small an area as possible.\nHowever, even this is speculative and could be ignored in case of emergency.\nZn addition, there. do not appear to be solid criteria. for determining\nthese limitations or restrictions,\nAs xnoxe data become available fxom simulations of orbiter \t\nreentry trajectories, glade path, and. landir_g (including sonic boons foot\xc2\xbb\nprints and instantaneous impact traces} for various orbitex inclarrations,\nit is expected that any linai.tations would be identified far the user.\nFollow\xc2\xbb yap effort is needed in behalf of the user.\n\n^\t\n\nf\n\n`\n\n\t\n\nSUMMARY SHEET FOR REFERENCE OR\xc2\xbb l\nSpace Shuttle Launch and Recovery Site Review Board Report\nTITLE:\t\nNone\nREPORT NO. ;\t\nDATE: .\t10 A.pxil 1 q72\nDx.
